levels being open, enclosed or integrated into the grade of the site.
Private Restricted Landing Area (PRLAS): A landing area for aircraft that is not
open to the general public and is restricted to use by the owner and those 
authorized by the owner. A PRLAS can be on land or water and include landing 
昀椀elds, heliports, and helistops. PRLAs are distinct from public airports and are not 
intended for commercial operations. 
Storage Facility
: A building or group of buildings consisting of individual, self-

and/or trailer units and other trucks, are parked or stored. The terminal may

FINANCE COMMITTEE PUBLIC HEARING AND DISCUSSION 
Ms. Amanda Loomis presented this article. This is a continuation of the clean-up and 
clarification of the Regulation and Use tables. While that project isn’t yet 100% complete, it’s 
getting close. In addition to clarifications and better usability, a major goal is to align Natick’s 
bylaws to the latest case law. 
Member Questions:
A member asked for clarification on the clean-up of residual “Reserved” notations, for items 
previously deleted via prior clean-ups. Ms. Evans, chair of the Planning Board, replied that 
they are confident at this point in the project that it’s safe to do so, and that the notations are 
no longer needed. 
A member asked about the location of a specific definition, and Ms. Loomis indicated that it’s 
elsewhere in the Zoning Bylaw.
108

--- Page 113 ---

Several members asked about the timing of this specific article’s hearing. What prevented it 
from being available for review/study way earlier in the hearing calendar? Ms. Evans 
explained that the ordering of the Planning Board’s work had been discussed, and that there 
wasn’t traction for making changes that would have distributed the review copies of the article
earlier, or allowed it to be scheduled at an earlier date. It’s an issue that will get continued 
attention. A member asked what other communities do with regard to the Planning Board to 
Town Meeting cycle. Ms. Loomis explained that it varies, but that there are opportunities for 
Natick to improve how it schedules work - including the Finance Committee hearing phase. 
Motions & Debate
Moved and seconded a motion to recommend Favorable Action. That motion passed by a 
vote of 10-1-0.
The committee voted 10-1-0 to recommend Favorable Action on the subject matter of 
Article 23.

II.C Use District Descriptions
1. West Central Corridor (WCC) Zoning District
The West Central Corridor (WCC) Zoning District is an integrated set of 
subdistricts located along major transportation routes. Its varied topography and
natural features create opportunities for diverse land uses while maintaining an 
appropriate balance among development intensity, ecological resources, and 
publicly accessible open space. The WCC District also encourages a mix of 
residential, retail, o昀케ce, and civic uses that activate the corridor and 
complement surrounding neighborhoods.
2. Limited Commercial (LC) Zoning District
111

--- Page 116 ---

The Limited Commercial (LC) Zoning District is intended as a transition zone 
along major transportation routes between larger commercial or industrial areas 
and nearby residential neighborhoods. It allows a mix of neighborhood-serving 
commercial uses and also permits mixed-use development that includes housing
integrated with those uses.

And further move that the T own vote to amend Section III – Use Regulations of the 
Natick Zoning Bylaws by deleting § III-D – Use Regulations for LC District in its entirety; 
and replace with a new § III-D West Central Corridor (WCC) Zoning District, to read as 
follows:
III-D West Central Corridor (WCC) Zoning District
1. West Central Corridor (WCC) and Subdistrict 
The West Central Corridor (WCC) Zoning District is a single district made up 
of 昀椀ve (5) subdistricts. Each subdistrict re昀氀ects its location along the corridor,
its relationship to surrounding neighborhoods, and the desired scale of 
development. Boundaries are intended to blend gradually rather than create 
sharp edges, and each subdistrict has tailored standards for building height, 
intensity of use, and design to promote coordinated, compatible development
across the corridor.
a. Subdistrict 5, West Central Hub (S5)
The West Central Hub Subdistrict (S5) functions as the corridor’s primary 
mixed-use center. It o昀昀ers the highest permitted building height and 
density in the WCC District and is located for convenient access to transit 
115

--- Page 120 ---

and other transportation options. Development in S5 shall create a 
walkable, pedestrian-oriented environment with active street-level 
frontages that accommodate a mix of uses, including retail, dining, o昀케ces,
entertainment and recreation, civic and educational facilities, and 
housing.
b. Subdistrict 4, West Central Connector (S4)
The West Central Connector Subdistrict (S4) serves as the corridor’s link 
between the Hub (S5), other subdistricts, and nearby neighborhoods. It 
accommodates mid-scale mixed-use development and is intended to be 
accessible by both vehicles and non-motorized travel. Development in S4 
shall maintain active, pedestrian-friendly street frontages and may include
a mix of commercial, service, and residential uses.
c. Subdistrict 3, West Central Residential (S3)
The West Central Residential Subdistrict (S3) focuses on predominantly 
residential development at a small- to mid-scale. It provides a transition in
height and building form between the mixed-use corridor (S4 and S5) and 
adjacent low-density neighborhoods, while maintaining pedestrian 
connections to nearby subdistricts and open spaces.
d. Subdistrict 2, West Central Single Residential (S2)
Reserved
e. Subdistrict 1, West Central Conservation and Open Space (S1)
The West Central Conservation and Open Space Subdistrict (S1) preserves
natural resources and open land while allowing limited landscape 
improvements for active recreation, passive enjoyment, and habitat 
restoration. Development is restricted to trails and supporting amenities. 
Most S1 land is held or protected by the Commonwealth of Massachusetts,
the Natick Conservation Commission, or a non-pro昀椀t land trust.
2. Use Regulations
a. A Lot in the WCC Zoning District may include two (2) or more uses as 
identi昀椀ed in § III-A.2 Use Regulation Schedule of these Zoning Bylaws.
b. All projects with a residential component shall c omply with § V-J 
Inclusionary Housing of these Zoning Bylaws. 
c. All projects S5 and S4 are required to include an a ctivated ground 昀氀oor by 
incorporating uses that encourage and support pedestrian activity, the 
Subdistrict, and abutting neighborhoods.  
i. Minimum Non-Residential Requirement for Residenti al Mixed-Use 
Developments
a) Buildings with a residential component in S5 shall  dedicate at 
least 昀椀fty percent (50%) of the ground-昀氀oor frontage facing a 
public way, or 昀椀fty percent (50%) of the building’s longest 
façade—whichever is greater—to non-residential uses. The 
designated non-residential space shall have a minimum depth of
twenty-昀椀ve (25) feet. Up to twenty percent (20%) of that non-
residential area may be used for residential-support amenities 
such as a lobby, leasing or management o昀케ce, co-working 
space, community room, or 昀椀tness center.
b) Buildings with a residential component in S4 shall  dedicate at 
least forty percent (40%) of the ground-昀氀oor frontage facing a 
public way, or forty percent (40%) of the building’s longest 
116

--- Page 121 ---

façade—whichever is greater—to non-residential uses. The 
designated non-residential space shall have a minimum depth of
twenty-昀椀ve (25) feet. Up to twenty percent (20%) of that non-
residential area may be used for residential support amenities 
such as a lobby, leasing or management o昀케ce, co-working 
space, community room, or 昀椀tness center
ii. The Planning Board shall con昀椀rm the required non -residential square 
footage at the opening public hearing for Site Plan Review and shall 
recon昀椀rm that 昀椀gure at the close of the hearing if building plans are 
amended during the review. The required non-residential area may 
be provided in a single building or distributed among multiple 
buildings on the same lot.
3. Intensity, Scale, and Design Regulations 
a. A Lot within the WCC Zoning District may have mor e than one (1) Building
or Structure per Lot.
b. The WCC Zoning District shall comply with § IV-B.3  Intensity Regulations of
these Zoning Bylaws.
c. T o facilitate smooth transitions between subdistricts, properties in S4 or S3
may apply the Intensity Regulations in § IV-B.3 to up to twenty percent 
(20%) of the portion of the lot that directly abuts another parcel in S5, S4, 
or S3. This 昀氀exibility is intended to allow a gradual change in building 
scale and bulk. Building-height limits may not be increased under this 
provision except by special permit from the Planning Board.
d. Design Standard Requirements 
Projects within the WCC Zoning District shall be designed to ensure 
coordinated building placement, streetscape and landscape treatments, 
and compatible massing at subdistrict edges, while meeting the standards
in § III-D.3 of these Zoning Bylaws.
e. Pedestrian Scale and Design Intent
Projects within the WCC Zoning District shall be designed to create a safe, 
attractive, and comfortable pedestrian environment along the public 
street frontage and within on-site walkways and open spaces.
i. Sidewalks and street frontages shall be sized, lo cated, and 
improved to accommodate the anticipated level of pedestrian 
activity.
ii. Buildings and structures shall be placed to main tain a consistent 
street frontage and reinforce a walkable public realm.
iii. Projects shall provide pedestrian-supportive am enities—such as 
benches, lighting, landscaping, bicycle racks, and way-昀椀nding 
elements—that bene昀椀t both site users and adjoining 
neighborhoods.
f. Major Transportation Corridor Design Intent
Projects within the WCC District that front on West Central Street, Speen 
Street, or Mill Street shall be designed in coordination with the 
Department of Public Works (DPW) on matters such as driveway and curb-
cut locations, sidewalk and streetscape design, drainage, and utility 
connections, and shall be consistent with applicable transportation plans 
to reduce vehicle trips and improve access to transit, pedestrian access, 
and bicycle facilities.
117

--- Page 122 ---

i. Street frontages shall be designed to encourage p edestrian activity 
and provide safe access to bicycle facilities, seating areas, pocket 
parks or open spaces, and similar streetscape features that support 
a walkable environment.
ii. Projects shall incorporate alleyways or pedestri an pathways, where 
feasible, to connect with adjoining properties and reduce local 
vehicle trips. If a physical connection cannot be established at the 
time of construction, the project shall reserve and clearly identify a 
future connection point to enable such linkage when adjoining 
properties redevelop.
iii. Projects shall coordinate the location and desi gn of bus stops, 
shelters, or other transit-supportive amenities with the appropriate 
transit agency and DPW to facilitate convenient use of the public-
transportation network.
iv. Non-residential and Residential Mixed-Use Develo pment projects 
with frontage on West Central Street, Mill Street, and Speen Street 
shall support non-vehicular modes of transportation through the 
design and construction of a multi-use pathway or sidewalk along 
the frontage of said project(s).
g. O昀昀-street Parking and Garages Intent 
In the WCC Zoning District, o昀昀-street parking shall be treated as a 
secondary, supporting element of the development rather than a 
dominant site feature.
i. O昀昀-street parking garages and surface parking are as shall be 
located to the side or rear of buildings so that primary street 
frontages remain pedestrian-oriented.
ii. Where a garage is located to the side of a build ing, its street-facing 
wall shall be set back so that it extends no more than ten (10) feet 
forward of the building’s front façade.
iii. All o昀昀-street parking lots shall be built with conduit and reserved 
space for transformers and switchgear to accommodate future 
installation of electric-vehicle (EV) charging stations in at least 昀椀fty 
percent (50 %) of the total parking spaces.
h. Architectural Design Intent
Projects within the WCC District—including Residential Mixed-use 
Development, non-residential, and Multi-Family Dwelling buildings—shall 
be designed to:
i. Break down the apparent mass of large or mid-scal e buildings 
through fenestration patterns, façade articulation, and other 
architectural techniques.
ii. Incorporate varied window styles, materials, and  façade details that
contribute to a pedestrian-scaled street frontage.
iii. Where a building includes podium parking that f aces a public street 
and has no ground-昀氀oor commercial use, the street-facing façade 
shall be treated with architectural elements—such as glazing, active
lobby space, or display windows—to maintain an activated street 
frontage.
i. Landscape and Open Space 
118

--- Page 123 ---

Projects within the WCC District shall incorporate landscape and open-
space elements that enhance the site design and provide transitions to 
neighboring properties.
i. Provide a combination of landscape features—such a s plantings, 
berms, walls, or fencing—to bu昀昀er the project while creating a 
gradual transition to adjacent properties.
ii. Plant a minimum of one (1) shade tree for every twe nty-昀椀ve (25) to 
thirty (30) linear feet of the building footprint.
iii. Plant a minimum of one (1) street tree for every t wenty-昀椀ve (25) to 
thirty-昀椀ve (35) linear feet of street frontage (excluding curb-cut 
widths) within the required front-yard setback.
iv. Where o昀昀-street parking spaces required by the Z oning Bylaws are 
not initially needed at the time of construction, the unused areas 
shall be reserved and may be improved as pervious pocket parks or
open-space areas until such parking is required.
v. Where a lot directly abuts the MBTA Commuter Rail  corridor, the 
project shall install and maintain a planted or landscaped bu昀昀er—
using a combination of vegetation, hardscape, and soft-scape 
materials—along the rear property line. The vegetated bu昀昀er shall 
be a minimum of ten (10) feet in width, located within the rear-
setback requirement of § VI-C. Bu昀昀ers shall be designed to reduce 
the apparent bulk and scale of buildings as viewed from the rail 
corridor.
j. Residential Outdoor Space, when applicable 
Projects with residential dwelling units in Subdistricts S5, S4, or S3 shall 
provide each unit with access to usable outdoor space, such as decks, 
porches, patios, lawns, roof decks, or pocket parks.

FINANCE COMMITTEE PUBLIC HEARING AND DISCUSSION 
Amanda Loomis, Director of Community & Economic Development and Terri Evans, 
Planning Board chair, presented this article. Ms. Loomis explained that this project area 
is now called the “West Central Corridor" as a better description. This primarily deals 
with the area along 135 in West Natick just south of the railroad tracks.
This area is currently broken up into multiple zoning districts. The Planning Board first 
tried to better understand what was going on in the corridor.  Once that was completed, 
the Board proposed creating several subdistricts that allows each parcel to be treated 
individually based on its actual use.  This is important here because of the varied 
topography in that area. Additionally, Ms. Loomis explained that there was a significant 
amount of input from property owners in the area - and they had recently sent out 1,200 
legal mailers. The input from property owners nearby emphasized the need for more 
walkability and helping small businesses.
The presentation walked the committee through each subdistrict, explaining its 
characteristics and components. There is a subdistrict designation specifically for 
Conservation and Open Space.
The town’s Comprehensive Master Plan, Natick 2023, focused on this area as a major 
opportunity for Mixed-use – especially in association with the West Natick train station. 
This zoning change requires contributions to a multi-purpose path on the north side of 
135 and requires connection between parking lots to cut down on curb cuts, as well as 
connectivity between alleys. The plan allows for site plan review for the commercial 
125

--- Page 130 ---

projects in this area, for which the Planning Board would be the review authority.
The is a smaller component of this article that deals with a small area located on Rt 27 
north of Rt 9. Four properties on Lenox St sit on lots which are split zoned. A strip of 
their lots is currently zoned Limited Commercial (LC), and are being rezoned 
Residential Single A (RSA). A small strip of Lowell Road, to the centerline is being 
rezoned from Commercial-II (C-II) to Limited Commercial (LC). 
Questions, Motions, and Debate:
A member asked about community engagement, and suggested that it had been 
insufficient to this point – having recently missed sending out updates to residents who 
had signed up. Ms. Loomis explained that there had been staff turnover, and that the 
Communications Director has been engaged. 
Members had questions about parking requirements, and how they will be calculated. 
Ms. Loomis said that an applicant would present a proposal based on industry 
standards and similar projects.  Then, the Planning Board would review that and get a 
peer review consultant if necessary. In her experience, applicants generally provide 
adequate parking, because it is difficult to lease a space without it. 
A member asked about noise abatement, especially considering that increased building 
size along the railroad track might reflect more noise towards residential areas than 
currently happens. Ms. Loomis explained the requirements for both hardscaping and 
landscaping as mitigation. The member asked about the MBTA installing noise barriers, 
which the Sponsor said they would look into. 
A member asked about the tree planting requirements, and some of the spacing details.
Ms. Loomis described the process, and explained that the spacing is specified as a 
range, because exact spacing requirements would run into issues with driveways, 
utilities, etc.
A member asked about the target density in the various subdistricts. Ms. Loomis 
directed the committee to the tables that define the specifications by district. The 
member had a further question about the need for a Conservation and Open Space 
designation. It was explained that it’s hoped that additional properties can be those 
areas, and having specifically designated areas will facilitate that. 
Questions & Comments from the Public
Mr. Brian Lucenta, Natick resident, asked about the expected population increase, and the 
impact on the schools and town infrastructure. He asked whether the Superintendent of 
Schools or the School Committee has had input into this. Ms. Loomis said that they have not 
– although they go under the assumption that the children would be dispersed throughout the
schools and grade levels without a major impact. The resident had a similar question about 
the Fire Department. Ms. Loomis explained that the Fire, Police, and Public Works 
departments had all participated in the planning. Mr. Lucenta explained that he graduated 
126

--- Page 131 ---

High School in 2004, and that, in his view, lots of other housing units have been added in 
Natick since that time.  He argued that this is going to raise costs for the schools significantly 
and we should take a pause before adding more housing.
Members moved and seconded a Referral to the Planning Board on Article 24 on 
Motions A and B.
Members also moved and seconded a recommendation for Favorable Action on 
Motions A and B. 
Members supporting Referral argued that the lack of notice to people who attended the 
summer 2024 meeting meant that there should be a further meeting to give people 
opportunities to attend and be engaged. Also there are people on Boden Lane who are 
extremely concerned about this development because of the traffic flow that this will 
create. They also noted the impact on the schools and town infrastructure. Another 
member expressed concern about the short time provided to review and study the 
documents. Several minor corrections were made, and he’s not confidant that additional
time wouldn’t have provided additional needed corrections. A different member noted he
thinks that the proposed subdistrict approach makes sense in this area, and that a lot of 
good thought had gone into the proposal – but that he feels that additional work is 
required. 
Members advocating a Favorable recommendation felt that, while not perfect, the 
proposal is very solid. One member noted living in the general area for years, and that 
the area is only getting worse. Improvements in more bike and pedestrian connectivity 
is a great aspect of this article, and it is an opportunity to have a revenue dense area in 
a place that is currently very vacant.
The Referral Motion failed 5-6-0.
The committee voted 6-5-0 on the Favorable recommendation. Since Natick bylaws 
require a minimum of 8 votes for a recommendation, the committee has No 
Recommendation on Motions A and B, 
On Motion C: The committee saw the value of this minor change, and voted 11-0-0 to 
unanimously recommend Favorable Action.
The committee has No Recommendation on Motions A and B, and voted 
Favorable Action the subject matter of Motion C of Article 24.
~~ END OF ARTICLE ~~

Section III-M. Accessory Dwelling Unit
1. Purpose and Intent 
An Accessory Dwelling Unit (ADU) is an alternative type of housing that allows 
Natick to expand and diversify its housing supply options without requiring 
additional land development. The design and implementation of an ADU is 
expected to 昀椀t on a Lot, with the 
Principal Residential Dwelling. 
2. Massachusetts General Law (MGL) 
a. ADUs are permitted As of Right per Massachusetts General Laws (MGL), Chapter (c) 
40A, § 3. 
b. The Town has established §III-M of these Zoning Bylaws to provide guidance and to 
reasonably regulate the construction of ADUs within single-family zoning districts. 
c. No ADU unit may be utilized as Short-term Rental.
d. The gross 昀氀oor area of the Principal Residential Dwelling or the ADU outlined in § III-
M of these Zoning Bylaws shall be calculated per 760 CMR 71.00, as amended. 
(Art. 20, Spring ATM, 05.06.2025)
3. Applicability and Requirements
a. In order to qualify for an As of Right ADU, the Principal Residential Dwelling and the 
Lot shall:
i. conform to the maximum Building Coverage of the respective Zoning District, per
§ IV-B Intensity Use Regulations of these Zoning Bylaws; and
ii. be the only residential Dwelling Unit on the Lot prior to construction of the ADU.
b. Neither the 
Principal Residential Dwelling nor the ADU shall be used as a Short-term
Rental.
c. Where there are two (2) or more ADUs on a Lot. One (1) of the ADUs shall be deed 
restricted as a昀昀ordable per and subject to § V-J Inclusionary Housing of these 
Zoning Bylaws.
130

--- Page 135 ---

d. The ADU and the Principal Residential Dwelling shall remain on the same Lot and 
shall not be divided onto two (2) separate lots. 
e. No ADU unit may be separately sold as a condominium, or otherwise.
4. Permitting Regulations and Permit Review Authority 
a. Building Permit Review – Building Permit Application 
The Building Commissioner shall be the permit granting authority for the creation of 
the following types of ADUs. Such review shall be conducted concurrently as part 
of a Building Permit application. 
i. A single ADU that is located entirely within the 
Principal Residential Dwelling or 
within an existing attached garage, with no exterior increases in square 
footage; 
ii. A single ADU located in an existing detached garage that is greater than ten (10)
feet from the Principal Residential Dwelling. Detached garages less than ten 
(10) feet from the Principal Residential Dwelling shall meet all Building Code 
requirements;
iii.   A single ADU to be located within a Principal Residential Dwelling at the time of
construction of the Principal Residential Dwelling; or
iv. The conversion of a Family Suite to an ADU, unless such conversion requires 
relief from the Zoning Board of Appeals (ZBA), and then either section c. or d. 
shall apply, depending on the facts and circumstances. Consultation with Town 
sta昀昀 is encouraged before any conversion application is 昀椀led.
b. ADU Site Review (As of Right) 
The Building Commissioner shall be the permit granting authority for the 
creation of the following types of ADUs. ADU Site Review shall be As of Right 
and limited to the bulk, height, scale, and placement of a single ADU on the 
Lot. 
i. A single detached ADU on a Lot located within the Allowable Building Envelope 
and greater than ten (10) feet from the Principal Residential Dwelling; 
ii. The construction of a single ADU on a Lot that is de昀椀cient only in Lot Area and 
Frontage; or 
iii. The construction of an addition for a single ADU that does not require relief from
the ZBA.   
c. ADU – Pre-existing Non-conforming Lots (As of Right)
The ZBA shall be the permit granting authority for ADU Site Review, which is an 
As of Right (majority-vote) review. The review of the following ADUs shall be 
limited to the bulk, height, scale, and placement of a single ADU on the Lot. 
i. The expansion of an existing Principal Residential Dwelling or existing building 
(that is greater than ten (10) feet in distance from the Principal Residential 
Dwelling) that is de昀椀cient in Intensity Regulations per §IV-B of these Zoning 
Bylaws; or
ii. The placement or addition of an ADU requires a Section 6 Finding beyond relief 
associated with Lot Area and Lot Frontage.  
131

--- Page 136 ---

d. ADU Special Permit
The ZBA shall be the Special Permit Granting Authority (SPGA) for the following 
Special Permits, which shall require a super-majority vote: 
i. The creation of two (2) or more ADU per Principal Residential Dwelling or Lot, 
which will require a super-majority vote.
ii. A Building proposed to exceed the allowable Lot Coverage or other dimensional 
requirements per § IV-B Intensity Regulations by Zoning District, with the 
exception of Lot Area and Lot Frontage. The Special Permit shall be required for 
the Building, not the ADU as a use.
5. Design Requirements and Findings
The permit granting authority or SPGA shall review an ADU application for compliance 
with the following Findings. 
a. The ADU shall be located in the Allowable Building Envelope of a Lot.
b. The Lot shall remain conforming to Building Coverage per §IV-B of these Zoning 
Bylaws for the respective Zoning District after the construction of an ADU.
c. An ADU detached from the Principal Residential Dwelling shall be a minimum of ten 
(10) feet from another Structure on the Lot, per the State Building Code.
d. A detached ADU shall not be located between a roadway and the designated 
frontage of the 
Principal Residential Dwelling. 
e. An ADU shall provide one (1) o昀昀-street parking space on the Lot when located a half
(1/2) mile or more from a commuter rail station. 
f. When an ADU has a separate exterior entrance from the Principal Residential 
Dwelling or is a separate Building, the ADU shall be accessible from a path or 
walkway.
g. The ADU is located on the Lot so it does not impede vehicular access and circulation
of the Lot, roadways, and sidewalks.
6. Review Procedures 
The ZBA shall evaluate each application based on the following respective Review 
Criteria. 
a. ADU Site Review Procedures 
For uses outlined in § III-M.4.c of this Bylaw, the following public review procedures 
shall be followed.
i. The ADU Site Review shall be posted on a public meeting agenda. 
ii. Notice of the public meeting shall be sent to abutters within three hundred (300)
feet of the boundaries of the Lot. 
iii. A decision shall be rendered within thirty-昀椀ve (35) days of the 昀椀rst public 
meeting.
b. ADU Special Permit Procedures 
For uses outlined in § III-M.4.d of this Bylaw, the review of such application shall 
comply with § VI-DD.1.e of these Zoning Bylaws.
Art. 20, Fall ATM 10/24/2024
132

--- Page 137 ---

.
FINANCE COMMITTEE PUBLIC HEARING AND DISCUSSION 
Ms. Loomis presented this article, and explained that the changes are required by the 
Attorney General’s office. In August 2024, a MA law was passed requiring ADUs as a right 
under Massachusetts law. In response, Natick developed and passed its ADU bylaw that fall. 
In the spring of 2025, the town passed an amendment to the definition of Gross Floor Area, 
based on earlier guidance from the AG’s office. 
Questions:
A member asked of the existing bylaw is in effect, in abeyance because of required 
corrections, or partially in force. Ms. Loomis explained that is partially in force.
Multiple members questioned certain aspects of ADU placement on a lot, especially as it 
relates to setbacks and height. Ms. Loomis explained that the current bylaw manages height 
and proximity differently than the AG’s revised requirements – and that ADUs must be of a 
height and placement matching the limitations of the primary structure to which they are 
accessory. This is less “neighbor friendly” than the bylaw Natick originally developed – but is 
one of the required changes.
Multiple members were quite adamant in their objections to having the issue dictated by the 
state. They stated that they are unable to vote in favor of an action they feel is coercive. 
A member moved to amend the motion, to add “or Bus Station” to the multiple places 
“Commuter Rail Stations”. The amendment impacts the requirements to provide a parking 
space. During discussion of the amendment, various concerns were debated: What is the 
definition of “Bus Station”? Does it require a permanent building? A passenger shelter? How 
does the MWRTA’s routes that operate by people waiving down the bus impact 
implementation? Multiple members felt that since the addition of “Bus Stations” was 
referenced in the AG’s letter, it should be addressed in the revisions. The amendment passed
(6-5-0). NOTE: since the Amended Motion ultimately received “No Recommendation” 
from the Finance Committee, the motion(s) presented here are the originals as 
submitted by the Sponsor. 
A motion was made and seconded to recommend Favorable Action, The motion received a 
vote of 6-5-0. Since the Natick bylaws require a minimum of eight votes for any 
recommendation, the committee has No Recommendation on Article 25.
The committee voted 6-5-0, and has No Recommendation on the subject matter of 
Article 25.

FINANCE COMMITTEE RECOMMENDATION
The Finance Committee 
took the following action:
RECOMMENDATION: Refer to Sponsor
QUANTUM OF VOTE: 9-4-0
DATE VOTED: 9/30/2025
MOTION (Requires a 2/3rds Vote)
Move that the Town vote to amend the Natick Zoning Map by rezoning 0 Worcester Street
(Assessor Map 23, Lot 69A) from Residential Single A (RSA) to Commercial – II (C-II).
FINANCE COMMITTEE PUBLIC HEARING AND DISCUSSION
Questions and debate by committee members surfaced repeated concerns about the impact 
of this rezoning on the nearby residential neighborhood in West Natick.  The lot coverage limit
would increase from its current level of 25% to 30%.  The building height limit would increase 
from 30 feet currently to 2.5 stories and 35 feet.  Concern was expressed that the resulting 
move of the boundary of residential zoning would shift the buffer zones called for around the 
commercial areas that are subject to agreements between Natick and Framingham, and that 
the shift would move those buffer zones closer to existing homes in the neighboring portion of
Natick.  This concern was disputed but not resolved to the satisfaction of some committee 
members.  There was also concern that the proposed new zoning could allow location of 
research laboratories  in close proximity to residences.

Article 26.

David Krentzmen, Vice Chair of the Community Preservation Committee (CPC) presented 
these motions alongside Amanda Loomis, Director of Community and Economic 
Development.  
The Sponsor explained the CPC’s process.  It starts with a letter of interest, then a review to 
see if it meets the criteria for spending CPA money on it.  If it does, it goes through a lengthy 
application process, after which it is voted on by CPC members.
Seven projects were presented, each individually having public hearings, discussion, and 
debate before the CPC.  The CPC is simply recommenders – it is ultimately up to Town 
Meeting whether to fund each project. They feel very confident in the decisions they have 
made as a committee that these projects are worthwhile for the town.

idea is to restore the cast iron fence that surrounds the library property. This is in the historic 
preservation bucket and was a unanimous recommendation.  The total project amount is

--- Page 150 ---

A member asked why the number here seems to have increased. Sharone Small, Director of 
Real Estate and Metrowest Collaborative Development explained that the overall costs of the 
project have gone up because of hazardous materials found in the building, as well as 
increased material costs.  The requests from the LOI to the application also went up because 
of the changes in certain tax credits.
A member asked how receiving funding from the CPC would help obtain grant funding for this
project. Ms. Small said that a showing of a local commitment to the project is a factor in the 
award of grant funding from the state. This funding represents only 3% of the total funding for 
this project and it would leverage approximately $30 in external funding per $1 of local 
funding. Another member asked how many more dollars of funding do the Sponsors 
anticipate asking for from the town. Ms. Small they have no plans to ask for more money as of
now.
A member of the public, asked whether this motion allows for the commitment but not the 
actual disbursement of funds until construction starts. Mr. Marshall explained that it would be 
through the contracting period that this would be figured out - but the conversations with staff 
are that funds would not be appropriated until they are needed. The member of the public 
further asked that we not fund something before the construction actually starts. The concern 
is that CPA funds will be disbursed and spent on early phases of the project, and if it doesn’t 
get completed, those funds will be lost without recourse. Mr. Marshall said the intent between 
the CPC and the applicant is that the funds wouldn’t be released to them until they got all of 
their project funding.  Could the language be added to this motion?  Yes, but I’m not counsel 
so I couldn’t say what that would say.
A different member of the public, asked whether the CPA funding came from transfers of 
property or real estate taxes. Ms. Loomis explained that CPA funds are collected from local 
real estate taxes after the first $100,000 of value (1%) plus a state match. The member of the 
146

--- Page 151 ---

public further said that including the assessed value of the property, plus the commitment of 
the Natick Affordable Housing Trust, Natick is up to nearly $6 million on this project. In the 
CPA documentation, it says that the local preference will only be consistent with state and 
federal law of 70%. She asked how this is calculated in terms of how many of the apartments 
are actually eligible. Ms. Small explained that the exact number of apartments will be finalized
when they receive funding but before construction on the project starts. For that reason, it is 
subject to change, but generally funding given through a project based voucher is not allowed
to get a local preference.  So, some apartments could be ineligible for the 70% local 
preference.

Mr. Jacobs, seconded by Ms. Monahan, moved that we recommend favorable action on the

Proponents of Favorable action argued that this is a once in a generation opportunity to 
increase our affordable housing stock significantly, and that we should support it.  Projects 
like this, where we can leverage CPA funds to get multiples more in funding from outside 
sources, were one of the big selling points of the CPA. They argued that it is hard to think of a
better value for our money - and further noted that this project has been through extensive 
processes in front of many other boards and committees and it would be unwise for the 
Finance Committee to attempt to derail it at this stage.
Proponents of Referral expressed concern that this project is going to cost Natick more 
money in the future and this town is strapped for cash. $750,000 can go to a lot of other 
affordable housing projects and all these things need to be cleared up before they can feel 
comfortable that this is “ironclad good and absolute.” Another Referral supporter explained 
that he had previously been a major advocate for affordable housing. However, he has grave 
concerns about this project. There are fundamental mechanics of the town that have not been
taken into account - such as the fact that the South Natick fire station can’t be replaced and 
the town is ignoring its obligation to do something there, but we are giving land away. That is 
a done deal, but now we are getting into the sunk cost fallacy as the numbers keep rising. 
This often happens with projects, but each time we do this, it’s an opportunity to re-assess 
this project. That member is unconvinced that the prior reviews were sufficient because 
nobody was looking at the big picture.
A member explained that she supports this project and thinks the increased costs are 
understandable given the increased costs of materials.  She also noted that the CPA has 
already collected multiples more money than this request and we are just getting started. 
Further there are no other affordable housing projects before us in this session and if you 
want to spend money on affordable housing, this is the way to do it.  Moreover, this has 
nothing to do with the override, because those are different sources of money.

Article 29 
Amend Zoning Bylaws: § V-H Signage and Advertising Devices - Downtown Mixed Use
(DM) and Center Gateway (CG) Zoning District
(Heather Rockwood et al)
ARTICLE LANGUAGE
To see if the Town will vote to amend the Natick Zoning Bylaws by amending § V-H Signage
and Advertising Devices, 2. Downtown Mixed Use District (DM) and Center Gateway (CG)
Signage; amending Section 200 - Definitions; amending § VI-K.4.d Signs and Advertising
Devices; and further amend any other associated sections, where applicable; or otherwise act
thereon.
PURPOSE OF THE ARTICLE
To amend the Natick Zoning Bylaws by amending § V-H Signage and Advertising Devices, 2. 
Downtown Mixed Use District (DM) and Center Gateway (CG) Signage; amending Section 
200 - Definitions; amending § VI-K.4.d Signs and Advertising Devices; and further amend any
other associated sections, where applicable; or otherwise act thereon.
FINANCE COMMITTEE RECOMMENDATION
The Finance Committee
took the following action:
RECOMMENDATION: Favorable
QUANTUM OF VOTE: 8-5-0
DATE VOTED: 9/30/2025
MOTION (Requires a 2/3rds Vote)
Move that the Town vote to amend the Natick Zoning Bylaw by adding new definitions for 
Individual Building Establishment Unit; Multi-tenant Building; Sign, Awning; Sign Box; Sign, 
Blade;  Sign, Building Identification; Sign, Directory Wall; Sign, Establishment Window; Sign, 
Exterior Notification Flag; Sign, Monument; Sign, Permanent; Sign, Projecting Hanging; Sign, 
Public Service; Sign, Roof; Sign, Sidewalk; Sign, Temporary; and Sign, Wall-Mounted, to read
as follows:
Section 200 – Definition
Individual Business Establishment Unit: A single physical unit that is leased or 
owned by a commercial business or organization that is used for commercial business 
operations.
Multi-tenant Building
: A Building that contains more than one (1) Individual Business 
Establishment Unit. 
149

--- Page 154 ---

Sign, Awning: A sign, also known as a canopy sign, located on the surface of an 
awning or attached above, below, or to the face of an entry canopy that identifies an 
Individual Business Establishment Unit or Multi-tenant Building. 
Sign Box: A predetermined, dimensional area of the first-floor exterior of a Building, as
approved by the Planning Board, in which a business sign must be located for an 
Individual Business Establishment Unit or Multi-tenant Building.
Sign, Blade: Similar to a Projecting Hanging Sign, but is a small, two-sided 
commercial sign that is attached to and projecting perpendicularly from the façade of a 
Building that identifies a commercial business located within an Individual Business 
Establishment Unit. Blade Signs may be stacked vertically. Information type is limited 
to the business name and logo.
Sign, Building Identification
: A sign that provides the recognized name of a Building, 
complex, or center, but not the name of a commercial business, Individual Business 
Establishment Unit, product, or service.
Sign, Directory Wall
: A sign that is located on an exterior wall, next to a publicly 
accessible entrance that is installed and maintained by a Multi-tenant Building owner to
identify commercial businesses and their location within the Building.
Sign, Establishment Window: A sign for a commercial business or organization that 
is located within an Individual Business Establishment Unit and is hung inside within 
three (3) inches or applied to the window itself. Such signage includes the name and/or
the logo of the business but shall not include the advertisement for specific products or 
services. 
Sign, Exterior Notification Flag
: A flag that notifies the public that a commercial 
business located within an Individual Business Establishment Unit is open or having a 
sale. Such flag shall be located on a pole that is no greater than four (4) feet in length, 
which includes the entire pole, bracket, and area where the flag is located. The pole 
shall be mounted to the exterior of a Building by bracket and not on the ground. Such 
signage shall only be displayed during hours of business operation and located as to 
not impede pedestrian mobility. 
Sign, Monument:
 A low-profile freestanding sign that is distinguished from other 
freestanding signs by its support structure. A Monument Sign is supported primarily by 
an internal structural framework or integrated into a solid structural feature, such as a 
masonry base or landscaping, rather than being held up by exposed poles. 
Sign, Permanent: A sign that is affixed to a Building, which includes the name and/or 
logo of a business(es) occupying an Individual Business Establishment Unit or name of
the Building. Such signage does not advertise a specific product or service and does 
not include a phone number or website address.
Sign, Projecting Hanging
: A two-sided commercial sign that is attached to and 
projecting perpendicularly from the façade of a Building that identifies a commercial 
150

--- Page 155 ---

business located within an Individual Business Establishment Unit. Information type is 
limited to the business name and logo.
Sign, Public Service
: A sign installed for the purpose of providing public information, 
wayfinding, or providing directions towards an indication of use not readily visible from 
the street (e.g., rest rooms, emergency services, etc.) and is managed by the Town of 
Natick. 
Sign, Roof:
 A sign that is erected on the roof or mounted to the uppermost horizontal 
architectural band of a Building to identify the name of the Building or commercial 
business. A Roof Sign is intended to be viewed by pedestrians and motorists from a 
distance and serve as an icon for the building.
Sign, Sidewalk
: Also known as a sandwich board or A-frame sign, is a portable, two-
sided commercial sign that is placed on the sidewalk to identify a business or advertise
daily specials or sales. Sidewalk Signs are intended to be viewed at close range by 
pedestrians on the same side of the street. 
Sign, Temporary
: A sign that is removed daily.
Sign, Wall-Mounted: A sign attached flat or mounted parallel to the facade of a 
Building that identifies a commercial business or organizational operations open to the 
public.
And further vote to delete § V-H.D.2 Downtown Mixed Use District (DM) and Center Gateway
District (CG) (sign regulations) in its entirety; and replace with a new § V-H.D.2 Downtown 
Mixed Use District (DM) and Center Gateway District (CG) (sign regulations), to read as

1. Permanent Signage shall not feature interchangeable letters, internal 
illumination, or include movable parts (exception for Barber Poles and 
clocks).
2. Permanent Signs are encouraged to display the Individual Business 
Establishment Unit street number unless provided on front door or

昀椀rst 昀氀oor Individual Business Establishment Unit, the 昀氀oor level where

withstand weather elements.

1. A maximum of one (1) Blade Sign front or side of a Building, 
located either on the corner of the Building or positioned in a 
location that does not impede with a 昀椀rst 昀氀oor Business 
Establishment; and

used to access the business establishment.
2. Blade Signs for non-昀椀rst-昀氀oor business establishments shall be

6. Wall-Mounted Sign
1. Wall-Mounted Signs are only permitted for 昀椀rst-昀氀oor Individual 
Business Establishment Units. 
2. Wall-Mounted Signs are prohibited from covering windows or 
architecture details.
3. No portion of a Wall-Mounted Sign is permitted to extend above 
the lower eave line of a building with a pitched roof; extend above 
the roof line or parapet wall of a 昀氀at roofed building; extend above 
the cornice or expression line of the base of a building.

1. Awning Sign may be located on the sloping portion of an awning 
are only permitted for storefronts where the typical area for a wall

ends.

8. Sidewalk Sign 
1. One (1) Sidewalk Sign is permitted per 昀椀rst 昀氀oor Individual

9. Monument Sign
1. Only one (1) Monument Sign shall be permitted for a Building, 
complex, or center.
2. The structure of a Monument Sign shall consist of brick or masonry
construction, complete with adequate footing.
3. The maximum height of a Monument Sign shall not exceed four (4)
feet to the top of the sign above the ground
4. A Monument Sign shall be located a minimum of ten (10) feet from
all property lines, and where deemed necessary by the Building 
Commissioner or the Planning Board, the setback shall be 
increased so that the sign and associated landscaping does not 
obstruct views from or onto other properties or site distance of a 
driver of oncoming, intersecting, or merging tra昀케c.
5. All Monument Signs shall be landscaped with well-maintained 
plantings ten (10) feet around the entire base of the sign.
6. Monument Signs are prohibited in the Downtown Mixed Use (DM) 
Zoning District.
(d) Sign Maintenance
1. All commercial signs,  including existing  nonconform ing  signs,
together with any supports, braces, anchors, and ot her supporting

(e) Permitting Procedures 
1. Permanent Signage 
1. Sign Box Permitting   
1. The Sign Box is a permitted location that holds a Permanent 
Sign that is approved by the Planning Board by a simple 
majority vote.
2. Modi昀椀cations that increase the size, modify the shape, or 
modify the location of an approved Sign Box, but proposed 
new Sign Box complies with § V.H.2 of this Bylaw shall 
require a new review and approval by the Planning Board.  
3. A Sign Box that requires dimensional relief or deviation of its
location from § V-H.2 of this Bylaw shall require a Special 
Permit from the Planning Board. The Design Review Board 
(DRB) shall provide a letter of comment to the Planning 
Board for such Special Permit. 
2. Established Sign Box Dimensions and Locations   
1. Permanent Signs that are placed within an approved Sign 
Box require a Building Permit.
2. Changes to the content of a Permanent Sign that complies 
with an approved Sign Box shall require a new Building 
Permit before installation.
2. Temporary Signage 
a)  The Select Board shall license Temporary Signage annually. 
b) A license shall include but is not limited to the location where a 
Temporary Sign can be located, requirements for use of a 
Temporary Sign, in addition to maintenance of condition of the 
Temporary Sign. 
3. Public Signage 
1. The Select Board shall be the approval granting authority for all
Public Service Signs and Directory Signs that are installed 
within the public right of way or on Town property for the 
purposes of way昀椀nding. 
2. Prior to review by the Select Board, the dimensions and location
of Public Service Signs and Directory Signs shall be reviewed by
the Police Department, Fire Department, Department of Public

--- Page 161 ---

Works, and the Building Commissioner to ensure that the 
signage does not compromise public safety and circulation.
3. Design associated with public way昀椀nding shall be developed 
through an open, public process. 
FINANCE COMMITTEE PUBLIC HEARING AND DISCUSSION 
The article was presented by the Executive Director of the Natick Center Cultural District.  
This article arose in the spring of 2025 when it was determined that A-frame signs are not 
allowed in the Downtown Mixed-Use and Center Gateway districts in Natick Center.  The 
Select Board granted a temporary special exemption to allow A-frame signs pending an 
amendment to the by-law governing signage.  The Director of Community and Economic 
Development stated the governing goals as giving businesses the ability to promote their 
presence while maintaining the desired character of Natick’s Downtown district.
Questioning and debate revealed broad support for businesses to be able to communicate 
their presence through signage in a manner that could contribute to the economic viability and
success of businesses in Natick.  The same discussion also focused on some concerns about
a compressed timeline and a reactive process.  One committee member expressed a desire 
to see more attention to distinguishing site review by the Planning Board from licensing by the
Select Board, and not to create an onerous process for applicants that mixes the two.

V-H. SIGNS AND ADVERTISING DEVICES        
D. Regulations and Restrictions Appliable to Use District
2. Downtown Mixed Use District (DM) and Center Gateway District (CG)  
           (a)  Accessory Signs
In Downtown Mixed Use District (DM) and the Center Gateway District 
(CG)  accessory signs that comply with the provisions hereinafter set 
forth are permitted.  All other accessory signs are expressly prohibited. 
(Art. 24, Spring ATM, 05/02/2023)
1.  Location:     
     The sign shall be a昀케xed to a building, except as here-in after provided.  A 
sign attached to a building shall be securely a昀케xed to one of the walls of 
the building and it shall be parallel with and not project more than six (6) 
inches from the face of such wall and shall not project beyond the face of 
any other wall of the building.
2. Size:     
Letters in a sign shall not be more than two (2) feet  in overall height,
exclusive of ascenders and descenders, with the tot al area of the
sign  not  to  exceed  thirty  (30)  square  feet.  (Art.  24,  Spring  TM,
4/13/2021)  
157

--- Page 162 ---

3. Number: 
There shall not be more than one exterior sign for each business, 
except that if the business has a direct entrance into the business in a 
wall other than the business front, there may be a secondary sign 
a昀케xed to such wall, and if the business has a  wall, other than the 
business front, that faces upon a street or parking area, there may be a
secondary sign a昀케xed to such wall whether or not such wall contains 
an entrance to the business; provided however, that no business shall 
have more than two secondary signs in any event. The width of the 
secondary sign or signs shall not exceed one (1) foot in overall height 
or 昀椀fteen (15) square feet in total area.  
In addition to the foregoing sign or signs, there may be one directory of 
the occupants or tenants of the building a昀케xed to the exterior wall of 
the building at each entrance to the building.  Such directory shall not 
exceed an area determined on the basis of one (1) square foot for each 
occupant or tenant in the building.
4.  Standing Signs:  
Standing signs are prohibited.

MOTION (Requires a Majority Vote)
Motion A for Changes to ByLaw Article 81 Chemical Storage Tanks and Systems
“Move that the Town vote to amend Article 81 of the ByLaws by:
adding the words “The word ‘fuel’ in the term ‘underground fuel’ shall be deemed to include
propane unless otherwise excepted herein.” at the end of Section 2 (a) so that Section 2 now 
reads:
“Section 2 Applicability
a. This By-Law shall apply to all underground fuel or chemical storage tanks and systems. 
The word ‘fuel’ in the term ‘underground fuel’ shall be deemed to include propane unless
otherwise excepted herein.
b. Storage systems in service at the time of the approval of this By-Law shall be brought
into compliance with the terms of this By-Law within ninety days of its approval.”
And by adding the words “This subsection 5 (a) (1) shall not apply to underground propane 
tanks.” at the end of Section 5 (a) (1) such that Section 5 (a) (1) now reads:
“Section 5 Tank Regulations
161

--- Page 166 ---

a) Every owner of an underground fuel or chemical storage tank and system shall file with
the Board of Health, the size, type, age and location of each tank and/or system and the
type of material stored, on or before ninety days after the approval of this By-Law.
1) Thereafter, every owner of a tank and system installation shall comply with the
provisions of Section 5A, hereof on or before January 1st of each succeeding year.
“This subsection 5 (a) (1) shall not apply to underground propane tanks.”
And by adding a new subsection 6 (f) as follows:
“f. Subsections 6 (c) , (d) and (e) of this Section 6 shall not apply to underground propane
tanks.”
And by adding a new subsection i as follows
“i. The provisions of subsection 8 (a) (1) and 8 (c) above shall not apply to underground
propane tanks.”
And by adding to Section 9 Tank Testing and Removal a new subsection (c) as follows:
“ c) This Section 9 will not apply to underground propane tanks.”
And by adding to (c) as follows:
“ c) This Section 10 will not apply to underground propane tanks.”
End of Motion A.
Motion B: Amending ByLaw Article 22 Town Counsel
Move that the town vote to amend Article 22 of the ByLaws by adding a new section 9 as
Follows;
“Section 9: Special Zoning Advisory Counsel
a. The Select Board, or other appointing authority as provided below, shall annually appoint
special zoning advisory counsel who shall be legal counsel with specialized knowledge
and experience in zoning and land use matters, including but not necessarily be limited to
relevant Massachusetts General Laws and case law. In addition to these qualifications
said counsel shall be responsible for becoming and remaining fully knowledgeable about
the Town’s Zoning ByLaw. Said counsel shall be an independent contractor to the town
and may be either associated with or unassociated with Town Counsel.
b. Said counsel shall assist and advise i) the Planning Board (including their staff support),
ii) the Finance Committee, iii) any standing committee created by representative Town
162

--- Page 167 ---

Meeting to review zoning articles and motions, iv) any special zoning study committee of
Town Meeting, and v) the Town Moderator if any such enumerated parties request and/or
require such assistance or advice concerning proposed changes to the Town’s Zoning
ByLaws. Such advice or assistance 1) may consist of analysis, drafting, review and /or
explanation of proposed changes to the zoning bylaw and 2) shall be in addition to the
role of town counsel. Said zoning advisory counsel shall answer such questions from
members of the Representative Town Meeting as the Town Moderator may direct and
may also recommend changes to the Planning Board and any committee, established by
town meeting, having a responsibility with regard to proposed changes in the Town’s
Zoning ByLaw.
c. After consideration of a recommendation, if any, that the Planning Board may timely
make, such appointment by the Select Board shall be made at least 60 days prior to fiscal
year end. In the event the Select Board does not so appoint such special zoning advisory
counsel, the Planning Board shall appoint such counsel at least 30 days prior to fiscal
year end. If the Planning Board does not so appoint such counsel, the Town Moderator
shall make the appointment. The judgement and evaluation of the qualifications for
appointing special zoning advisory counsel shall be the decision of the appointing
authority.
End of Motion B.
FINANCE COMMITTEE PUBLIC HEARING AND DISCUSSION 
Motion A:
Mr. Griesmer, Chair of the CBRC explained that last spring they were in front of the 
committee with a motion related to propane storage, but it had some issues that they had to 
fix so now they are bringing it back.
This change would regulate the placement of such tanks underground by the Board of Health 
(BOH), which has an interest in regulating these to protect ground water etc. This change 
adds propane to the definition of “fuel” in the by-law that regulates underground fuel tanks to 
ensure that the BOH can regulate them. However, they don’t want to have propane tanks 
subject to the same regulations as oil and gasoline storage, so it is put in one section of the 
by-law but exempt from the rest of the by-law.
A member asked what is being protected; is it about protecting against contamination from 
the fuel? Mr. Griesmer said that it is about protecting against contamination in part but the 
purpose language doesn’t preclude the by-law accomplishing other goals. Another member 
explained that water that can unnaturally pond because of these underground tanks, which 
can lead to contamination even if the contamination is not from the propane in the tank itself.
The committee voted unanimously to approve the Motion A (13-0-0).
163

--- Page 168 ---

Motion B:
Mr. Griesmer explained that this was also in front of the committee in the Spring. There were 
some fatal technical issues with that motion that required correction. This proposal would 
create an appointed town counsel specifically for zoning issues. The Sponsor explained that 
in his view, we need this because of the high volume of zoning by-laws that go on the warrant
– an average of 14.5 a year. Zoning is quite complicated – to do good zoning, you have to 
have a good idea and concept, and it must be framed and drafted in words that are very 
technically refined and then inserted into the by-law in such a way that fits in terms of things 
like cross-references and definitions. If mistakes are made in a zoning by-law, someone can 
build a building that hangs around for 70-100 years, which is a serious consequence 
compared to other by-laws that are more easily fixed. Town Meeting members are not 
(generally) zoning experts, nor is the majority of the Finance Committee.
The Sponsor further explained that we have had any number of really good zoning ideas that 
have had wording problems that qualify as outright errors. Some of them don’t get caught until
there is a problem, while others get caught on Town Meeting floor - which isn’t ideal. With 
something this important, we need to provide a standing resource to the town who can
assist anyone who wants assistance with this including the Planning Board, the Finance
Committee, and Town Meeting.
Some examples of things that, in the Sponsor’s opinion, were problematic: there was a 
marijuana zoning by-law that had dozens of errors in it which required multiple changes to fix; 
we had a cleanup of the Special Permit Granting Authority aspect of the by-law which put all 
districts on a list - but the Downtown Mixed Use zone inadvertently got left off the list. This led
to three or more projects that were to be built without proper legal authorization; we had a 
cluster by-law that had so many issue we had to place a moratorium on it and then fix it; last 
year, Natick passed the ADU zoning by-law and half of it did not get approved by the AG; an 
error in the Special Permit procedures that were revised in 2024. While caught, there was an 
incorrect cross-reference which could have eliminated most requirements to get a special 
permit.
Questions from the Committee:
A member questioned the term of the appointment, and how differing appointing “windows” 
impact the ability of the primary and secondary appointing authorities ever to coordinate 
terms. The Sponsor indicated that partial term appointments can be made in order to 
synchronize future appointment terms and calendars. The general rule that legal counsel can 
only be appointed for single year durations also somewhat simplifies this coordination.
Multiple members questioned the need for additional counsel outside Natick’s existing 
appointed Town Counsel firm. Current counsel has multi-disciplinary legal staff, and it seems 
simpler to ask for additional legal support from within existing counsel. The Sponsor indicated 
that there’s no reason that this additional, dedicated zoning counsel couldn’t come from within
the currently appointed firm, but that it would need to be an additional, separate resource to 
get around bottlenecks and resource constraints.
A member asked what prevents town administration from asking for an additional resource 
from the current town counsel’s firm. The Sponsor indicated that there’s nothing preventing 
that, other than funding. The intent of this bylaw change is to compel the hiring of additional zoning 
164

--- Page 169 ---

specialty counsel, and to direct the administration to budget the additional appropriation needed. 
Multiple members asked questions related to costs of this change. The Sponsor indicated that it’s 
difficult to project, since it would be dependent on the amount of time budgeted, used, and the 
contract/retainer terms. Discussion continued trying to nail down an estimated budget, without 
additional clarification. A member asked what the future funding source would be, to which to Sponsor 
responded tax levy, as are the other budgeted legal line items. 
Multiple members questioned what discussions the CBRC has had with other stakeholders - such as 
the Planning Board, Select Board, ZBA, Town Administrator, etc. The Sponsor indicated that there has
not been any recent discussions, and that most prior discussions indicated that the other stakeholders 
were not in favor of this proposal - sometimes strongly disagreeing with the reasoning and intent. 
Several members indicated a strong preference that this change be openly discussed with the various 
stakeholders, and that the outcome of those meetings be presented. 
Motions and Debate:
There were two motions made, seconded, and debated. The first motion was for Referral to the 
Planning Board, Select Board, and Town Administration. The second motion was to recommend 
Favorable action. 
Debate supporting Referral centered on a couple of themes: Members felt that, while problems have 
certainly existed (some serious), the mechanisms already exist for engaging specialty zoning counsel 
- and additional bylaw changes are unnecessary. Many members also strongly feel that the process of
engaging the other stakeholders and discussing the range of concerns is an important part of the 
process - and that it hasn’t happened. It was also noted that the intended outcome might not be 
realized if all stakeholders aren’t willing to engage/consult with this additional resource - and that it 
might be wasted funding. The lack of a firm range for the potential expense was also concerning. 
Members supporting a Favorable recommendation focused on this change as a potential solution to 
the long standing problem of errors, omissions, and drafting problems making it to Town Meeting 
deliberations - and in some cases, into the bylaws. These issues can cause serious, long lasting 
problems if implemented, and expend a very large amount of time and resources even when they 
don’t make it all the way to implementation. The time various boards, committees, and Town Meeting 
spends on corrective activity is of great concern. 
Voting:
The Referral Motion failed 5-8-0.
The Motion to recommend Favorable Action failed 6-7-0
The committee voted 13-0-0 to recommend Favorable Action on Motion A, and has No 
Recommendation on the subject matter of Motion B, for Article 31.
~~ END OF ARTICLE ~~

Motion B
“Move that the Town vote to amend Section 1 Rules of Procedure of Article 3 of the bylaws by

“In addition to any statutory requirements for public hearings in advance of consideration of

before the meeting and that no vote should be taken except as otherwise provided in this 
Section 1. When mandatory conditions precedent have not been met, allowable motions are 
postponement to a time certain or no action.” and by inserting the words 
“In addition,” before the words “The Representative Town Meeting” in the last sentence of the 
current text of said second paragraph and changing the “T” in the word “The” of said sentence
to lower case so that the second paragraph of Section 1 now reads:
“In addition to any statutory requirements for public hearings in advance of consideration of 
articles by Representative Town Meeting, the public hearing held by the Finance Committee 
on all matters of business within the warrant for any town meeting pursuant to Article 23 
Section 4 of these bylaws also constitutes a required mandatory condition precedent to 
consideration by Representative Town Meeting. When the requirement of a mandatory 
condition precedent has not met, the Moderator should rule that the motion is not properly 
before the meeting and that no vote should be taken except as otherwise provided in this 
Section 1. When mandatory conditions precedent have not been met, allowable motions 
under these bylaws are ‘postponement to a time certain’ or ‘no action’. In addition, the 
Representative Town Meeting may, by a 2/3 rds vote, waive any requirement in the bylaws for
a public hearing, unless required by statute or the Town Charter, on specified or particular 
matters of business included within the articles of any Town Meeting.”
End of Motion B

--- Page 173 ---

by Johnson, Trustman, and Wadsworth” and inserting the words “ Town Meeting Time, A 
Handbook of Parliamentary Law, 4 th Edition published by the Massachusetts Moderators 
Association” so that the first paragraph of said Section now reads:
Section 1 Rules of Procedure
The proceedings of Town Meetings shall be governed by such rules of practice as are clearly 
set out in “Town Meeting Time, A Handbook of Parliamentary Law, 4 th Edition” published by 
the Massachusetts Moderators Association except as modified by law or by the following 
sections.
In the event that “Town Meeting Time” does not clearly indicate a single course of action, the
ruling of the Moderator shall prevail.”
End of Motion D.
FINANCE COMMITTEE PUBLIC HEARING AND DISCUSSION 
Paul Griesmer, Chair of the Charter and Bylaw Review Committee (CBRC), presented this 
article.  He explained that our current bylaw references the current edition of “Town Meeting 
Time”, and there is a new, 4th edition out.  The CBRC was asked in July by the Moderator to 
take a look at the new edition and compare relevant sections in the old version to the new 
version. 
The main areas where differences were noted: (1) the new version does not require the 
seconding of motions.  It says if your practice was always to have motions seconded, you 
169

--- Page 174 ---

should continue that, but if it isn’t seconded, the Moderator “may” allow a motion to die for 
lack of a second. To make it clear that Town Meeting does want to have motions seconded, 
the CBRC is proposing adding that to our bylaw.  A second is a small but easy check to make
sure we aren’t having to debate anything anyone wants to make a motion about. 
(2) Natick’s current bylaw requires the Finance Committee to hold hearings on each Article 
that comes before town meeting.  That is our current bylaw which was done against the 
backdrop of the old version which said that if any public hearing is not held, the Moderator 
should as a matter of procedure, not allow it.  However, ⅔ of Town Meeting can waive the 
hearing requirement.  The new edition of “Town Meeting Time” changes it to “a public hearing
required by statute” which doesn’t include our bylaw.
Finally, the 3rd edition says a Motion to Dissolve is not in order as long as any article is not 
disposed of.  The 4th edition says that the motion to dissolve is not in order for any article that
has not been acted upon or passed over, which would be a change from our existing practice.
So, we want to preserve our existing practice.
Finally, Motion D changes the reference from the floating reference to any edition to a 
reference to the specific new edition.
Questions from the Committee
A member asked whether the new approach to dissolving changes the components of de 
facto dissolution because of failure of quorum. The Sponsor answered “No”, because it only 
impacts the motion to dissolve, not failure to have a quorum.
A member asked what the Moderator’s view is on these suggested changes. Jeff Alderson, 
Town Moderator, said that he is the one who brought this to the CBRC’s attention.  He has 
read this new edition in detail, and  is actually quite happy with a lot of good things that have 
been added about accessibility and hybrid town meeting - so he’s very excited we’ve made it 
this far to review this.  He thinks the CBRC did a great job with their review and he likes this 
set of motions.  He said the Massachusetts Moderator’s Association suggests adopting the 
“floating pointer” to the name of the book rather than which edition, but he thinks the proposed
approach is better and more cautious.  In summary, he supports all four motions. Mr. 
Alderson further indicated that Town Counsel would also be reviewing it as part of the general
review of all articles.
Motions and voting:

unanimously (13-0-0).

Article 33
A Moratorium on Artificial Turf
(Donna McKenzie et al)
ARTICLE LANGUAGE
To see if the Town will vote to enact a moratorium on artificial turf fields; or otherwise act
thereon.
PURPOSE OF THE ARTICLE
To enact a moratorium on artificial turf fields; or otherwise act thereon.
FINANCE COMMITTEE RECOMMENDATION
The Finance Committee
took the following action:
RECOMMENDATION: A: No Action
B: No Recommendation
QUANTUM OF VOTE: A: 10-0-0
B: 6-4-0
DATE VOTED: 9/18/2025
MOTION (Requires a Majority Vote)
MOTION A
Move that Town Meeting adopts a moratorium on artificial turf by adding a new Section # 
(number of bylaw) to the General Bylaws of the Town of Natick entitled, “Moratorium on 
Synthetic Artificial Turf,” that reads, “The construction of synthetic or artificial turf on outdoor 
athletic fields located on Town land shall be prohibited for a period of three years from 
January 1, 2026-December 31, 2028. This Bylaw shall not apply to the installation or use of 
synthetic/artificial turf in indoor facilities. An indoor facility is one that has a permanent roof 
enclosed by permanent non-removable, non-retractable walls and doors. This Bylaw will not 
affect the use or maintenance of the existing three artificial turf fields at Cole Athletic Field 
(Operated by Parks and Recreation, 179 Boden Lane), Memorial Field (Natick High School, 
92-108 West St.), and Kennedy Middle School (165 Mill St.). This moratorium may be 
extended upon its expiration by a majority vote of Town Meeting.”
MOTION B
Move that Town Meeting adopt an advisory for  all town departments to abide by a three-year 
moratorium on the construction of artificial turf fields on town property for a period of three 
173

--- Page 178 ---

years from January 1, 2026-December 31, 2028. This Advisory shall not apply to the 
installation or use of synthetic/artificial turf in indoor facilities. An indoor facility is one that has 
a permanent roof enclosed by permanent non-removable, non-retractable walls and doors. 
This Advisory will not affect the use or maintenance of the existing three artificial turf fields at 
Cole Athletic Field (Operated by Parks and Recreation, 179 Boden Lane), Memorial Field 
(Natick High School, 92-108 West St.),  and Kennedy Middle School (165 Mill St.). This 
Advisory on a moratorium may be extended upon its expiration by a majority vote of Town 
Meeting.
FINANCE COMMITTEE PUBLIC HEARING AND DISCUSSION 
Motion A: The Moderator, in consultation with Town Counsel, determined that Motion A is out
of scope, and can not be taken up by Town Meeting under this Article’s language.
Motion B: Dr. Donna McKenzie, citizen sponsor, presented this article, along with co-
sponsors Dr. Roger Luckman, Tien Lum, and Ed Devereaux.  They made several points in 
favor of this advisory resolution:
 Some studies have shown negative health impacts related to the microplastics that are
used in artificial turf.
 These microplastics also create serious environmental concerns, including PFAS 
contamination of water supplies.
 Artificial turf may be more expensive than natural grass when you factor in installation 
and replacement costs.
 This would not prevent the building of a turf field, but rather would allow a “thoughtful 
pause” so the community as a whole can take a deep look at this issue before deciding
how to move forward.
A group of members of the public opposed to the moratorium resolution.  That group included 
Julie McDonough, a former school committee member and member of an internal working 
group created by Natick Public Schools1 to look into this issue.  Ms. McDonough made 
several points against the resolution:
 There is a great need for additional field space in Natick.
 A turf field will provide year around playability, and many more hours total of usable 
field time.
 A turf field will be less expensive to maintain.
Many other communities use turf fields for sports.
The process required for building any new field will already be thorough and include 
review by all the relevant stakeholders.
1 Elise Gorseth, Chair of the School Committee, noted that (as of the date of the Fincom hearing) the School Committee 
had not yet reviewed this issue.
174

--- Page 179 ---

Several members of the public spoke both for and against the proposed resolution, largely 
mirroring the arguments above.  Some also argued that turf fields create more potential injury 
risk, while others argued that the risk of injury was similar to grass. 
Motions & Debate
Members of the committee made arguments that also largely mirrored those above, with 
some additional commentary about procedure.  Some argued that an advisory moratorium 
strikes a good balance because it does not require the town to do anything differently but 
does send a message that the public would like a close look at this before moving forward.  
However, one member argued that an advisory moratorium was unnecessary because we 
can have this conversation regardless, as the discussion that evening reflected.
The committee voted 6-4-0 on a motion to recommend favorable action on the subject matter 
of Article 33, Motion B.  Because our by-laws require a majority vote of the entire membership
of the finance committee (8 out of 15), to make a recommendation, that means the Finance 
Committee has no recommendation on Article 33, Motion B. 
 
The committee voted 10-0-0 to recommend No Action on the subject matter of Article 
33 Motion A, and has No Recommendation with regard to Article 33, Motion B.
~~ END OF ARTICLE ~~

money to be expended under the direction of the School Building Committee for the Memorial 
Elementary School building project, currently located at 107 Eliot Street, Natick, Massachusetts,
for which feasibility study the Town may be eligible for a grant from the Massachusetts School 
Building Authority./ The MSBA9s grant program is a non-entitlement, discretionary program 
based on need, as determined by the MSBA, and any costs the Town incurs in connection with 
the feasibility study in excess of any grant approved by and received from the MSBA shall be the
sole responsibility of the Town; or otherwise act thereon.//

Article  32
Changes in Town Meeting Time and Town ByLaws
(Charter and ByLaw Review Committee)
To see what action the Town will take to amend or to change:
1) Section 1 Rules of Procedure of Article 3 Procedure at Town Meetings of the Town ByLaws to
change the title and authorship reference to <Town Meeting Time, A Handbook of 
Parliamentary Law,  4th Edition= (the 4th Edition) or to a more or the most  recent edition of 
Town Meeting Time updated by the Massachusetts Moderators Association;
2) Section 1 Rules of Procedure of Article 3 Procedure at Town Meetings to specify whether 
references to Town Meeting Time shall automatically refer to the most recent edition of Town 
Meeting Time or to provide that such versions, if any, subsequent to the 4th Edition, must be 
specifically named and or adopted by Town Meeting vote; 
3) Other sections or Articles in the Town Bylaws provided such amendments or changes are 
directly related or responsive to changes in the 4th Edition that are i) identified, ii) noticed, iii) 
highlighted and/or contained within a 4th Edition chapter or section that is listed in either a) the 
<Town Meeting Time 4th Edition Appendix C: Mapping of 3rd Edition to 4th Edition Sections= or 
b) the  <Editorial Notes on Town Meeting Time 4th Edition= as such documents are published 
by the Massachusetts Moderators Association with a copy of each document placed on file 
with the Town Clerk of Natick; 
(For convenience only, copies of documents referred to in a) and b) above may also be found at 
https://massmoderators.org/tmt4/ under

the Post Office in said Natick, and at the following public places in said Natick, to wit:  Precinct 1: 
Reliable Cleaners, 214 West Central Street; Precinct 2: Cole Recreation Center, 179 Boden Lane; 
Precinct 3: Kennedy Middle School, 165 Mill St.; Precinct 4: 2 Summer Street; Precinct 5: Wilson 
Middle School, 22 Rutledge Road; Precinct 6: East Natick Fire Station, 2 Rhode Island Avenue; 
Precinct 7: Lilja Elementary School, 41 Bacon Street; Precinct 8: Natick High School, 15 West 
Street; Precinct 9: Community Senior Center, 117 East Central Street; and Precinct 10: Memorial 
Elementary School, 107 Eliot Street.

posted in the Natick U.S. Post Office, Town Hall, Bacon Free Library and Morse Institute Library
seven days at least before October 21, 2025; also by causing the titles of the Articles on the Warrant
for the 2025 Fall Annual Town Meeting to be published once in the Newspaper called "The
MetroWest Daily News," with notice of availability of an attested copy of said Warrant, said
Newspaper published in the Town of Natick and said publication to be August 29, 2025.
Hereof fail not and make due return of this Warrant with your doings thereon to the Town Clerk at or
before the time appointed for holding said meeting.
Given under our hands this 25th day of August 2025.
